==PAGE:1==
 
I 
 
Implementing a framework for the utilization of 
big data in decision-making 
 
Mahsa Eskandarzadeh s192933 
 
 
Master thesis 
Supervisor: Kathrin Kirchner 
Kongens Lyngby - July 2021 
 
Department of Technology, Management and Economics  
Technical University of Denmark 
 
==PAGE:2==
 
I 
 
Abstract 
Getting started on the path to digitalization is a new approach that has involved many companies 
and forced them to implement data-driven frameworks for their decision-making processes. 
Organizations identified data as a powerful asset for providing a unique opportunity in terms of 
competitive advantages. Despite that recognition, previous research has indicated that many 
companies are still struggling to implement data-driven decision-making successfully. The main 
reason for their failures was much attention towards technology. However, it should be noted that 
these tools will not be valuable without humans as users responsible for interpreting data and using 
them for decision-making. The focus of existing data-driven decision-making frameworks is on 
the needed processes for performing data-driven decision-making. They do not have any solution 
for potential challenges during the implementation phase. Therefore, it is imperative to investigate 
the barriers in the implementation phase. To address this gap, this study aims to propose a 
framework for organizations that have struggled in the implementation phase of data-driven 
approaches. This study aims to investigate the barriers from the perspective of decision-makers 
and find solutions for identified challenges. To answers, the research questions, qualitative analysis 
by a comprehensive literature review and a series of semi-structured interviews among the 
manager of a manufacturing company in Denmark was conducted.  As a result, it was identified 
that all challenges and enablers are related to four dimensions: Data, process, implementation, and 
people. Furthermore, it was discovered that lack of alignment between data strategy and business 
strategy leads to challenges in all dimensions such as data veracity in data dimension, data 
architectures and integration in process, data governance, and analytics capabilities in 
implementation. Moreover, it was identified that personal characteristics of decision-makers such 
as competencies, data literacy, related education, and years of experience had a significant impact 
on the use of data-driven approaches. The proposed framework is helpful for any company to help 
them towards the implementation of data-driven decision-making. For applying the proposed 
framework, it is necessary to evaluate the company's current situation in the implementation phase 
to find out the challenges and, based on them, apply the needed dimensions of the framework.   
 
Keywords:  Data-driven decision-making, Big data utilization, Decision-makers, digitalization  
==PAGE:3==
 
II 
 
Resumé 
Mange virksomheder er involveret i digitalisering tilgang hvilket tvinger dem til at implementere 
data-driven frameworks for deres beslutningsprocesser. Virksomheder har identificeret data som 
en god mulighed der kan give en fordel i konkurrencedygtighed i marked. Dog, de fleste 
virksomheder har problemer med at implementere data-driven system i deres virksomheder. 
Selvom de fleste kender til dette issue, er der stadig en del virksomheder har stadig ikke kendskab 
til deres problemer for at kunne implementere data-driven i beslutningsproces fase. Hovedsagelig 
grund kunne være de er mere teknologiske orienteret. En af vigtigste punkter er at disse værktøjer 
ikke er nyttige uden at have de rigtige folk der kan benytter dem i beslutningsprocesser. 
Tidligere framework af beslutningsprocesser har fokus på manglende den nødvendige proces for 
at udføre beslutningsprocesser hvor der ikke er rigtige præsenteret nogen form for løsninger i 
implementering fase. Derfor, er det oplagt at undersøge de relevante barrierer i denne fase. Målet 
med dette projekt er at forslå et framework for virksomheder der har problemer i implementering 
fase af data-driven tilgang. Ydermere, dette projekt undersøger barrerier for beslutningstagernes 
perspektiv og finder løsninger for identificeret udfordringer. For at kunne svare på 
undersøgelsesspørgsmål, er der foretaget kvalitativ analyse ved hjælp af en omfattende 
litteraturgennemgang og en del interview med managere af en Dansk virksomhed. 
Resultaterne viser at de identificerede udfordringer er relateret til 4 dimensioner: Data, Proces, 
implementering og mennesker. Ydermere, det har vist sig at fejljustering mellem data strategi og 
business strategi kan resultere i at disse nævnte dimensioner oplever udfordringer såsom 
pålideligheden i datadimension, dataarkitekturer og integration i proces, datastyring og 
analysefunktioner i implementering. Desuden, har det vist sig at beslutningstageres karakterisering 
såsom kompetences, datakendskab, uddannelse og erfaring har væsentlig indvirkning på brugeren 
af data-driven metode. Den præsenterede framework kan være nyttigt for alle virksomheder der 
har problemer med at implementere data-driven beslutningstagere. For at kunne implementere 
dette framework, er det vigtigt at kende virksomhedens nuværende situation i implementering fase. 
Dette kan hjælpe med at finde udfordringer i denne fase hvor de nødvendige dimensioner kan blive 
taget i brug.

CHAPTER 1 
 1 Introduction 
 
CHAPTER INTRODUCTION 
This thesis studied data-driven decision-making in manufacturing companies, focusing on 
helping managers achieve data-driven decision-making (DDDM). This chapter consist of: 1.1 
Background,1.2 learning Objections,1.3 Problem Formulation,1.4 Case study description,1.5 
Purpose statement and research question, and 1.6 Outline of the Thesis. 
 
1.1 Background 
Provost and Fawcett defined that data-driven decision-making as "the practice of basing 
decisions on the analysis of data rather than purely on intuition" (Provost and Fawcett 2013). 
Today, contemporary companies strive to gain more information and increase their choices by 
using data-driven decision-making approaches to be more efficient. The way companies make 
decisions is radically changing. If decisions were taken on a manager's 'intuition and gut instinct' 
in the past, organizations increasingly use database analytics to help decisions. The intention, 
preparation, data literacy, and strategic implementation plans are required to extract value from 
data. Companies in almost every sector are concentrating on using data for competitive advantage. 
With the aid of large quantities of data, this modern decision-making can provide valuable insights 
if enabled by the right technical and organizational resources. The manufacturing sector is now 
moving towards a new industrial revolution called Industry 4.0 or smart factory. (Sjödin, et al. 
2018). This phenomenon is defined as the digitization of the manufacturing company and was 
defined for the first time in 2011 by the German government. (Sjödin, et al. 2018). These 
researchers argued that the biggest challenges in adopting this new approach are the touch skills 
==PAGE:13==
 
2 
 
and qualifications of the employees: the collection, processing, and visualization data. (Sjödin, et 
al. 2018). 
Consequently, the way they make decisions has been modified by many managers in 
manufacturing firms. Instead of relying solely on intuition, managers who make decisions based 
on the experiences plan to rely more on data to obtain more reliable information, defining 
important insights, forecasting the future based on the knowledge from the data, and use them in 
their decision-making processes (Brynjolfsson and McElheran 2016a). However, because of the 
vast volume of recent data, organizations need specific techniques to synthesize this knowledge to 
find its value (Chen, Chiang and Storey 2012).  
Therefore, it is still critical to determine whether the managers, infrastructure, and culture 
in these companies are prepared for Data-Driven Decision-Making. Hall et al., in their article, 
stated that the focus of the existing model for data-driven decision-making is to the complete 
process of decision-making (Hall, Jia and Song 2015). These models show the sequence of the 
processes required to make a decision based on data ( (Davenport, Harris and Morison, Analytics 
at Work: Smarter Decisions, Better Results. 2010), (E. Mandinach, et al. 2008), (Hall, Jia and Song 
2015)). Therefore, there is a gap in these models in that they did not provide a solution to support 
potential challenges in implementing the processes.   
 In addition, decision-makers require a specific range of skills and abilities in executing 
DDDM, including data literacy, data analysis, reporting, and visualizing the outputs (Dykes 2017). 
Hall et al., in their article, proposed the needed capabilities for implementing data-driven decision-
making (Hall, Jia and Song 2015). However, the gap in their analysis is that they did not mention 
how to implement these capabilities and deal with potential challenges in the implementation phase. 
Another critical step is making trust between the produced knowledge from data and 
decision-makers, which is directly related to the nature of the data. Pak and Desimone argued that 
one of the most critical factors for implementing DDDM is building trust in data-driven decision-
making by carefully convening decision-makers to learn about the principle and values they will 
get from DDDM. Because DDDM can be intimidating for decision-makers who do not have data 
analysis experiences, providing feedback on DDDM can help investigate the barriers and find a 
solution for them. 
Additionally, implementing a feedback mechanism will encourage them to learn the needed 
skills (Pak and Desimon 2019). Carrillo et al. (2019) completed Pak and Desimone's idea. They 
==PAGE:14==
 
3 
 
stated that turning decision-makers into data-driven decision-makers requires developing a "pro-
analytics" approach that helps connect the various analytical concepts and technologies and trust 
data analytics results for decision-makers. This transformation calls for a cultural shift into 
business analytics and statistics (Carrillo, et al. 2019). It is imperative to define how decision-
makers make more data-driven decisions and utilize data in their decision progress. They need to 
make more productive and effective decisions that consider resource constraints while optimizing 
their profit margins. An element of this is to ensure a high focus on data literacy amongst people 
in the organization. When stakeholders are high on data literacy, they will be more able to 
communicate the value of their handling data.  
Another justification is based on the global economy's high growth, which leads decision-
makers to accelerate decision-making speed. This makes it possible for decision-makers to have 
access to "actionable data," which is essentially data that can be used in a "real-time" way 
(Hedgebeth 2007). This involves the availability and use of data to increase the decision-making 
process's efficiency, reduce external and environmental uncertainties, and incorporate a higher 
degree of rationality in the decision-making processes (Provost and Fawcett 2013). Ultimately, the 
purpose of implementing a framework for data-driven decision-making is to facilitate decision-
making strategies based on scientific evidence and organizational capabilities. Organizational 
capabilities refer to the maturity of data and decision-makers' analytics skills.  
The literature claimed that advanced analytics provides significant benefits to businesses, 
but many businesses meet challenges in the implementation phase (Cech, Spaulding and Cazier 
2018). Most companies start implementing data-driven approaches by investigating technology, 
hiring skilled people, and providing business intelligent (BI) tools (Ugarte 2021). Most problems 
come up in the implementation phase when people must start to use the new tools. As the amount 
of data collected increased every day, the role of data management and preparation becomes more 
important. Provost and Fawcett (2013) stated that for implementing data-driven decision-making 
successfully, two critical components are: i) Data science and ii) Human. These researchers stated 
that most of the struggles in the implementation phase come from the human side. Ugarte stated 
that most companies think the challenging part is developing new tools, and it is easy to ask people 
to use the developed tools (Ugarte 2021). However, having high technology system without users 
could not make any value. 
==PAGE:15==
 
4 
 
Therefore, this study's practical value proposes a framework for organizations that have 
struggled in the implementation phase of data-driven approaches. This study investigates the 
barriers from the perspective of decision-makers and recommended solutions for identified 
potential challenges in the implementation phase. Therefore, it is necessary to overview big data 
characteristics, decision-making processes in organizations, types of decisions, and decision-
makers' characteristics. In addition, the theoretical background will be investigated in these 
concepts. Although a wealth of literature suggests that advanced analysis offers significant benefits 
for corporations, many cannot perform because they fail to establish a framework supporting data-
driven decision-making (Ugarte 2021).  
1.2 learning Objections 
This research explores and defines the barriers to implementing the data-driven 
approaches for decision-makers and finding a solution for the identified challenges. Therefore, 
the learning objective of this research are: 
• List and describe relevant literature within the data-driven decision-making 
implementation. 
• Interpret and assess the existing framework for data-driven decision-making. 
• Performing an empirical data analysis based on the interviews with decision-makers 
for identifying the challenges of implementing data-driven decision-making. 
• Assess the potential and reliability of solution proposals by discussing the identified 
challenges and making recommendations for them. 
• Propose a framework for utilizing data in decision-making processes by using the 
design science approach. 
• Comparing the proposed framework with the existing framework 
1.3 Problem Formulation 
This study addresses the organization's general problem of implementing a framework for 
utilizing data in decision-making. Previous research indicates that despite the popularity and 
success of data-driven decision-making, many companies and organizations are still struggling to 
implement a data-driven decision-making framework successfully (Ugarte 2021). Currently, much 
emphasis is placed on technology rather than humans in charge of analyzing data and making 
==PAGE:16==
 
5 
 
decisions ( (Miragliotta, Sianesi, et al. 2018) (Ugarte 2021) ). According to the literature, the most 
common barriers that prevent implementing data-driven decision-making are the lack of data 
literacy of the decision-makers, the lack of change strategy, the capabilities needed to implement 
a DDDM, and the cultural impact of the DDDM.( (E. Mandinach 2012) (Dunn, Airola and Garrison 
2013) (Dykes 2017) (Ugarte 2021)).  
This study investigated to define the struggles for companies that already use data-driven 
approaches (Partly or not very successful), which means that they already have a framework for 
implementing data-driven approaches. However, in the implementation phase, they are not 
successful as expected. Increasing the amount of data in terms of velocity, variety, volume, veracity, 
and value has a practical impact on data quality. Moreover, the new approaches to data-driven and 
digitalization lead to change in the essence of the decisions, which made it more complicated for 
decision-makers due to the increase in the number of factors influencing the decision-making 
processes.  Accordingly, encouraging the decision-makers to switch from the intuition-based 
decision to DDDM is a complex process. Furthermore, many factors have influenced the success 
of implementing a DDDM framework. Therefore, companies that were not successful in 
implementing a DDDM approach need to have a framework covering all aspects of data-driven 
decision-making approaches and recommend a solution based on the identified challenges. The 
gap of the existing framework is that they are suitable for companies that want to start the 
digitalization journey and did not define any tools to identify the challenges during the 
implementation. The existing frameworks show the process steps of data-driven approaches and 
do not clarify the potential challenges during the implementation. Therefore, this study proposes a 
framework for companies that have failed in the implementation phase.  
 The purpose of this qualitative study is to review the existing frameworks and essential 
concepts in defining a framework for implementing data-driven decision-making at a 
manufacturing company in Copenhagen by defining the current situation of the implementation of 
data-driven approaches and identified challenges. Furthermore, the impact of decision-makers' 
characteristics on data-driven decision-making will be investigated.  
1.4 Case study description 
Implementing a DDDM framework for manufacturing companies is essential because 
many manufacturing companies have a strategy to switch to Industry 4.0. Digitalization needs to 
==PAGE:17==
 
6 
 
make changes in the technology, processes, and people. Therefore, a single case study based on 
qualitative data would be carried to investigate the purpose statement. The case study is in Danfoss 
-climate department, one of the world’s leading suppliers of innovative and energy-efficient 
solutions.  The Service Innovation team has recently formed an analytics & Insights team that’s in 
the process of building new services as part of Danfoss’ AlSense IoT Cloud. They are interested in 
both strategy and vision for evolving data-driven solutions. One of their main goals is to find an 
organizational setup needed to accomplish this vision and how Danfoss can better utilize data-
driven decision-making as internal value creation. Some pilot meeting with the Analytics and 
Insights team manager has been carried out to find out more information about the company's 
current situation in data-driven approaches. The analytics and insights team manager stated that 
they already implemented the data-driven approaches. However, in the path of evolving data-
driven solutions, they are looking to identify the struggles of using data to know how 
accomplishing this vision. The analytic and insight team manager stated that they meet some 
problems that people do not trust to data, and they are looking to find out the reason and how to 
solve it. The following realities have been identified based on the pilot meetings during the study: 
- 
Danfoss has the technology and the infrastructure for implementing data-driven 
approaches. 
- 
Danfoss already implemented some steps of the DDDM framework, such as collecting 
data, organizing data, extracting data /making data insights and, analyzing data. 
- 
The people of Danfoss have an awareness about the implementation of data-driven 
approaches in the company. 
- 
The main problem is that people do not trust data. 
1.5 Purpose statement and research question 
According to the background and problem formulation, this thesis proposes a framework 
for utilizing big data in decision-making in manufacturing industries that already implemented 
data-driven approaches but have failures in the implementation phase and providing 
recommendations and alternatives for the case mentioned in section 1.4. 
 
The main question is: 
==PAGE:18==
 
7 
 
How achieve data-driven decision-making approaches in manufacturing companies 
which has challenges in the implementation phase of data-driven approaches? 
The sub-questions are:  
1. What are the potential challenges in implementing data-driven decision-making 
approaches? 
2. How engage decision-makers and promote a data-driven mindset? 
 
1.6 Outline of the Thesis 
The theoretical framework is discussed in Chapter 2.  It consists of: 2.1 Big data analytics, 
2.2 Decision-making process in organizations, 2.3 Data-Driven Decision making (DDDM), 2.4 
Data-driven decision-making approaches, 2.5 Decision makers characteristics and culture, and 2.6 
Summary of the Literature. The research methodology employed in this thesis is described in 
Chapter 3.  The theoretical and empirical results are analyzed concerning the thesis's described 
study questions are presented in Chapter 4. By synthesizing the findings and conclusion discussed 
in Chapter 5, Chapter 6 accomplishes the thesis's References, and Chapter 7 involved the 
appendices.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:19==
 
8 
 
CHAPTER 2 
 2 Theoretical Framework 
CHAPTER INTRODUCTION 
Big data analytics must be interpreted as dramatic for conventional decisions by 
investigating how data-driven strategic insight will redefine technical resources in the multiple 
stages to improve process effectiveness. Existing research suggests implementing a fundamental 
orientation to handle big data across the decision-making cycle: data-driven decision-making 
(DDDM). (E. Mandinach, A perfect time for data use: Using data-driven decision 2012) (Hall, Jia 
and Song 2015)The element of data-driven decision-making are data, analytics, decision, decision-
making process, and decision-makers. This chapter presents the theoretical findings. Section 2.1 
Big data analytics describes big data characteristics and analytics approaches. In section 2.2 
Decision-making process in organizations, the organization decision-making process and types of 
decision-making are introduced. Section 2.3 Data-Driven Decision making (DDDM) aims to 
differentiate the data-driven decision-making process based on programed and non-programed 
decision making, structured and unstructured data, and how to position them in decision-making 
quadrants. Section 2.4 Data-driven decision-making approaches describe the different approaches 
and frameworks of data-driven decision-making. 2.5 Decision makers characteristics and culture . 
Finally, Section 2.6 Summary of the Literature reviewed the findings from the literature and 
introduced the identified elements for designing the interview questions. 
 
 
==PAGE:20==
 
9 
 
2.1 Big data analytics 
Digital data have many benefits, such as being easy to upload, reproduce, and reusable. 
However, big data cannot immediately gain a competitive advantage because various variables 
such as data usability, accessibility, efficiency, heterogeneity, data processing management skill, 
attitude, and perspective towards data may affect their beneficial usage. (Torisi, et al. 2018). 
Organizations can handle and collect data more efficiently through the rising diffusion of big data 
analytics. By reviewing the scholars' literature's, the three central perspectives defined for big data 
analytics: First, big data characterize by complying with the 5 «Vs.», which are simply five 
characteristics of this technology:  
1)Volume means the size of data.  
2)Velocity indicates the speed of creating/processing/ analyzing,/ storing data.  
3)Variety indicates different data types, sources, and modes to handle them.  
4)Veracity indicates quality, reliability, and uncertainty in data.  
5)Value indicates discovering actionable knowledge, high return on investment, increased 
relevancy to customers or products, or innovations in business operations/processes (Song and Zhu 
2016).  
When more companies continue to harness the power of Big Data, the number of V's has 
expanded: Viscosity: Refers to how complex the data is to use or integrate, Volatility: Refers to 
how often data changes occur and, therefore, how long it is valid (Cupoli 2015). One of the biggest 
challenges in warehousing and analytics solutions is data loading, modeling, cleansing, and 
analytics. Massively parallel computing or parallel processing and distributed data solutions 
approached these problems. However, they have much broader consequences (Cupoli 2015). The 
data sets' size involves improvements in how data is processed and obtained, and interpreted. 
Figure 1 presents a visual overview of the variety of data that has been accessible across Big Data 
technology and the ramifications for data storage options. 
==PAGE:21==
 
10 
 
 
Figure 1: Data storage challenges (Cupoli 2015) 
 
The second perspective defined big data analytics as new analytical technologies and 
business possibilities. It includes analytical results and a broader range of methods that turn big 
data into practical knowledge and develop a competitive advantage. The tremendous availability 
of heterogeneous and large data exceeds conventional storage and processing tools by posing a 
complicated data management topic (Provost and Fawcett 2013). Thus, extensive data analysis 
should be based on the deliberate evaluation of the best analytical methodology. Hence, it is 
essential to derive substantive knowledge and context from data and achieve strategic benefit from 
its use, beyond implementing data analysis algorithms to have a total data perspective (Gandomi 
and Haider 2015). What capabilities are involved depends on the position in the process of data-
driven decisions. The data-driven decision-making approach may be viewed as a supporting aspect 
to make intuition-based judgments more concrete. Decision-makers should therefore be able to 
analyze data, domain knowledge, and the intention to exercise the wisdom provided by these data 
to implement those decisions. Decision-makers must turn data in perspective and insight into 
==PAGE:22==
 
11 
 
decision-making and value decision-making so that organizations reap competitive benefits. 
(Gandomi and Haider 2015) 
The third approach to data processing in a modern world, which scientists and industry 
have been working on since these days, has been called the 'data life cycle,' which refers to the big 
data architecture. Digital data provides many benefits, such as being easy to upload, share, 
reproduce, and reusable.  However, to exploit all the benefits, as Bharadwaj and et al. (2013) 
proposed, data must be properly collected, handled, and analyzed beyond standard software 
analytical tools' technical and rapidity capacities (Bharadwaj, et al. 2013). Therefore, the 
implementation of a big data architecture would consider the various problems inherent with this 
paradigm. The following: scalability, availability of data, integrity, data transmission, consistency 
of data, producing the correct metadata to classify the source of data and the mechanisms used to 
achieve its traceability across the data life cycle( data provenance), big data management system, 
data heterogeneity (structured and unstructured, with varying frequencies of time) (Blazquez and 
Domenech 2018).  
The data life cycle is the series of phases that the data completes from the moment they 
entered the system until they were eliminated from the system or deposited (Simonet, Fedak and 
Ripeanu 2018). After reviewing other literature in the data life cycle Troisi (2020) accomplished 
the big data life cycle based on the following phases: 1) collection; 2) organization; 3) extraction; 
4) integration; 5) analysis; 6) sharing; 7) storage; 8) reuse. These phases, as illustrated in Table 1, 
should be reread using a complete data perspective to incorporate data analysis into standard 
management processes (Troisi, et al. 2020)   
Table 1: The main steps of big data management. (Troisi, et al. 2020) 
Main data analysis phases 
Main management phases 
Data collection 
Establishment and sharing of a cohesive data-oriented culture 
Data organization 
Selection of an integrated set of analytics in line with the strategic 
goal 
Data extraction 
Adequate technological infrastructure 
Data integration 
Computing skills 
Data analysis 
Analysis and research skills, Management’s ability to interpret 
results in line with the strategic goal and to catch opportunities 
Data sharing 
Data report and diffusion 
Data storage 
Feedback collection 
Data reuse 
Renewal of the knowledge acquired for continuous improvement 
==PAGE:23==
 
12 
 
 
If the data collection method and criteria have been established, the data collection process 
continues at various analytical stages. There are three main types of analytics maturity, each with 
its market advantage and level of difficulty in obtaining it (Trindade, Ochoa and Freitas 2016) :  
1) Descriptive analytics ( what has happened?): The foundation of every research software 
is descriptive analytics. This approach aims to consider how the future is deduced based 
on the results.  
2)  Predictive analytics ( what will happen?): Uses links between historical data and past 
computing and incidents to forecast the future.  
3) Prescriptive analytics ( what should happen?): Uses quantitative analyses of real-time 
data to forecasting potential system behavior. 
2.2 Decision-making process in organizations 
Many scholars explain that decisions are always based on several levels and factors. They 
suggested that decision-makers need to take different viewpoints and facets of an issue before 
determining( (Cronje, Ferreira and van Antwerpen 2017) (Espinoza, Proano and Villegas 2019)). 
Decision-making is a process of collecting data based on goals and KPIs, analyzing and using 
patterns and facts from these findings to build strategies and practices that support business in 
various fields, and subsequently selecting the "best" option (Simon 1997). The meaning of "Best" 
is the best approach to the specific issue of choice. The decision-making method varies in features 
and is thus addressed by various strategies (Lu and Yan 2019). In a simplistic context, complex 
judgments are widespread and inherent in human experience, in which an individual, group, or 
entity chooses among various paths to obtain the desired result. Decision challenges are different, 
ranging from regular organizational decisions to corporate decisions on long-term strategy and 
from sole internal decisions to multi-level decisions or multi-organizational decisions (Lu, Zhang 
and Ruan 2007).  Rabin and Bazerman (2019) stated that there is two classification system for 
decision-making based on two different types for collecting and analyzing information: Intuitive 
and Rational.   
• Intuition Decision-Making is "a vague sensation" or "holistic thinking, immediate 
insight, seeing the answer without knowing how it was reached" or "a technique to 
quickly recuperate knowledge from previous experiences and forms of 
knowledge." (Ritter and Dijskterhuis 2014).  
==PAGE:24==
 
13 
 
• Rational Decision-Making involves relevant evidence review and a systematic 
process that takes time and commitment. (Fitzgerald, Mohammed and Kremer 
2017) 
The importance of keeping consistent decision-making and being cautious about too risky 
is expressed by Rabin and Bazerman (2019). This kind of protection can help prevent poor, 
unadvised decisions. However, organizations are looking for ways to implement data-driven 
decision-making out of fallible humans' hands. (Pranjic 2018). Lu and Yen (2019), as illustrated in 
Figure 2, proposed a technical framework of Traditional and data-driven decision-making to clarify 
the basic concepts of decision-making. The main difference between traditional and data-driven 
decision-making is the data and learning model added between problem and final solution. In 
traditional decision making the decision model is based on the experiences of the decision-makers. 
In a data-driven decision-making model, the decision model and learning model are based on data. 
 
Figure 2: The technical framework of data-driven decision-making and traditional decision making (Lu 
and Yan 2019) 
Another approach in decision-making models is types of problems and decision makings. 
For the first time in (1960) Simon distinguished the decision-making process in two polar types 
programmed and nonprogrammed decisions. Programmed decisions deal with simple, familiar, 
==PAGE:25==
 
14 
 
often-occurring problems with well-established and well-informed solutions. These decisions are 
taken in regular, repetitive, well-determined circumstances, decision-making rules based on habits, 
established policies, procedures, or computational techniques. However, the nonprogrammed 
decision is a complex problem and a non-recurring decision that never occurred before. (Simon 
1960) 
 Therefore, the decision-makers do not have any specific procedure or solution requiring a 
custom-made solution. Structured problems are common and easily defined, and unstructured 
problems are defining as new problems that are ambiguous. (Kudyba 2014). Accordingly, Desouza 
and Jacob indicated that DDDM works better on programmed decision-making. ( Desouza and 
Jacob 2014) These researchers stated that DDDM could be made the process of programmed 
decision-making faster with more quality. 
 
Majumdar (2010), in his article, mentioned that programmed decisions are taken at lower 
levels of management, and nonprogrammed decisions are generally taken at higher levels in the 
organization. (See Figure 3) 
 
Figure 3: Type of problems, decisions and level in organizations (Majmudar 2010) 
 
While decision-making at the highest level has the most considerable effect, strategic 
decisions and those involving the most significant accountability, tactical and operational decisions 
can inform the frontline staff's strategic choices. This means that operational decisions in an 
aggregate manager can affect the course of strategic indicators. (Basic and Aleksic 2018).  
==PAGE:26==
 
15 
 
One of the recent models for decision-making provided that innovation and cognitive 
science could significantly improve decision-making capabilities were proposed by Abdallah et al. 
(2019), both in intuition and rational decision-making. These researchers model stated that any 
decision-making process consists of four consecutive steps:  
 1) The interpretation of input from the environment. 
 2) The processing of interpreted information. 
 3) An (intuitive) optimization process that prioritizes the choices and determines the best 
alternative.  
 4) Make a decision that expresses itself in choosing the best alternative through a particular 
course of action (Abdallah, Halima and Ibtissem 2019). 
According to these researchers, the cognitive models concentrate on expectations and values and 
anticipate intentions and behaviors. Human efforts, particularly dynamic activities such as new 
venture initiation, new production product design, programmed and nonprogrammed decision-
making, are the product of people's cognitive processes. Humans can think of potential future 
outcomes, determine which is more important and whether it is possible to accomplish these 
outcomes. (Abdallah, Halima and Ibtissem 2019).  
Firm agility is an essential quality of a business organization to improve the internal 
decision-making ability to respond to the unpredictable and fast changes in the business and 
technology external environment (Darabos-Longin 2018). This ability is partially accomplished 
by technical advances, like Business Intelligence solutions(BI), Big Data strategies, and supporting 
technology that facilitates connectivity and cooperation to improve the effectiveness of strategic 
plans and decision-making processes. (Darabos-Longin 2018) 
2.3 Data-Driven Decision making (DDDM) 
Domains such as data management, data collection, and data quality have become 
increasingly relevant for organizations since they can use their data to make more informed 
decisions. Gandomi & Haider (2015)and Brynjolfsson (2016a) stated that big data analytics make 
value for businesses when organizations use it in their decision-making processes to improve the 
quality of the decisions based on the data. Modern organizations also aim to use data-driven 
strategies to support their decision-making at different levels. (Pranjic 2018). This leads the 
organization to rationality in its decision-making processes. Data-driven decision-making 
(DDDM) does not only relate to numbers and data. The goal is to allow the unrefined raw data to 
==PAGE:27==
 
16 
 
become an actionable insight by converting them into usable knowledge (E. Mandinach, et al. 
2008). Provost and Fawcett (2013) and Mandinach (2012) proposed that the two critical 
components to the implementation of DDDM are "(i)data science concentrates on understanding 
data processing phenomena through concepts, methods, techniques, and (ii) human capabilities." 
Also, Mckinsey proposed that best-in-class data science is an iterative approach leveraging 
innovative practices and human resources experience. Mckinsey illustrated in Figure 4 that the 
"man + machine" approach for data enrichment is still perfect in the current world. The specialist 
who uses their expertise to justify their conclusions must involve data scientists. This includes 
translating existing data into new variables by data analytics skills. (Mohr and Hürtgen 2018) 
 
Figure 4: Best-in-class data science approach. source: Mckinsey.com 
It is essential for efficient and successful decision-making to have the correct data and 
resources (Davenport, Harris and Morison 2010). Mandinach (2012)and Grover (2018) concluded 
that companies need not only high-quality results but also qualified data management systems, 
analytical software, human analytical talent, and finally, data-driven culture to produce valuable 
knowledge and observations that are useful for decision-making and being a data management 
organization (DMO). Mandinach (2012) stated that data literacy is one of the fundamentals of 
DDDM. Organizational stakeholders should align with the values guiding operational and strategic 
management while developing a data-driven policy culture. 
Seddon and colleges proposed a process to achieve the maximum potential of data-driven 
solutions, consist of 1) analytics; 2) insights; 3) decisions; 4) actions (Seddon, et al. 2017). Data-
driven process roles in decision-making are a supporting tool to provide evidence to prove a 
==PAGE:28==
 
17 
 
position but do not often take decisions by themselves. Lu and Yan explained that inherent rules 
and knowledge could be derived from data and specifically used to produce decision-making 
solutions (Lu and Yan 2019). These researchers argued that the core principles and strategies in 
data-driven decision-making and cluster-related advances in the technological area are 
systematically explored into two main categories: programmable data-driven decision-making (P-
D3M) and non-programmable data-driven decision making (NP-D3M) (Lu and Yan 2019). The 
first category (P-D3M) takes two main phases toward decision making:  
1) Optimization model, It requires a decision-making model focused on data mining or 
statistics and an adequate formulation of a programmable model;  
2) Includes designing solution algorithms to achieve an optimal solution to the resulting 
model of decision. (Lu and Yan 2019) 
The steps for implementing a typical P-D3M method are summarized in Table 2. 
Table 2: The steps for Implementing P-D3M (Lu and Yan 2019) 
Steps 
Descriptive methods 
Predictive methods 
1 
Problem definition and formulation, parameter identification 
2 
Data collection, preprocessing, and partition 
3 
Measure selection for descriptive analysis 
Learning methods and 
architecture selection 
4 
Parameter estimation by summarizing what happened 
Parameter estimation by 
predicting what will happen 
5 
Testing using the goodness of fit tests and residual 
examination 
Testing generalization 
performance using new data 
6 
Using the prediction result to update the decision model 
 
The second category (NP-D3M) naturally applies to computationally infeasible or 
prohibitively costly situations for deriving the decision model. To address this issue, NP-D3M 
explores machine learning algorithms that discover data rules and defines the relationships 
between the input data, internal and output variables for making decisions directly, enabling 
decision-makers to strategize based on data-based evidence. (Lu and Yan 2019) 
In addition to different types of decisions, organizations are an encounter other challenges 
related to big data. For example, gathering data from different sources leads to structured and 
unstructured data (Kudyba 2014). Structured data can be interpreted immediately by the computer 
system, while unstructured data are typically non-numeric and can never be measured without 
==PAGE:29==
 
18 
 
previous transformation.1 (Intezari and Gressel 2015).  Their investigation was on designing a 
knowledge management system (KM) to implementing a systematic process for creating, sharing, 
and implementing knowledge in organizations. Advanced knowledge systems relate to a specific 
KM system that helps an organization incorporate big data into its repository of information and 
knowledge.  
These researchers introduced knowledge as: “person’s own experiences, perceptions, 
preferences, perspectives, values, and beliefs. “ They stated that it is possible to control the 
uncertainty and ambiguity for strategic decisions by implementing an advanced knowledge system. 
They stated that integration between big data and knowledge leads to produced expert insight and 
makes effective strategic decisions. 
2.4 Data-driven decision-making approaches 
The grasp needs to provide innovative business models that can gradually discern how to 
work with data at each stage of business decision-making and turn data into knowledge. 
Competitive advantage leads to a data-driven approach in management studies (Troisi, et al. 2020). 
Dunn et al. (2013) stated that focusing on culture, technology, and data is the most prioritized 
aspect of implementing DDDM approaches. Implementing data-driven decision-making needs the 
active role of leadership in promoting an innovation-oriented community and the careful 
commitment to data processing in each decision-making phase.  After the transition to the 
implementation phase, the challenges are turned into cultural issues. Many researchers argued that 
culture is the greatest obstacle to effective deployment when trying to transform human behavior. 
One of the most important decisions is determining what automation tools are needed to enable 
workers to access and perform data science and to what extent data literacy should be 
accomplished before starting their use. 
Davenport et al. (2010) proposed a framework to transform data into knowledge and 
improve an individual's analytics capabilities. The model can be applied at different levels of an 
 
 
1 Examples of structured data are purchase order data, product IDs and quantities, customer IDs 
and click streams. Examples of unstructured data are customer reviews, calls, chats, sounds, transcripts, 
social networking, blogs, forums, emails, images, colors and shapes (Intezari and Gressel 2015) 
==PAGE:30==
 
19 
 
organization and helps develop ongoing capabilities in a particular business segment. As shown in 
Figure 5, This model includes three main parts: context, transformation, and outcomes. 
1) Context: Decisions are taken within a specific business plan, a specific set of knowledge 
and skills, a specific culture and organizational structure, and a specific set of technological 
and data capabilities. 
a) Strategy: The clearer and more comprehensive a company's business plan, the more 
apparent the data and analytic capabilities it needs. 
b) Skills and Experience: The degree of the particular skills and experiences for 
transforming data to knowledge depends on the individual roles and responsibilities.  
c) Organization and Culture: Davenport et al. stated in their research that the most 
significant barriers for an organization to improving capabilities are structuring analytic 
resources and developing a data-oriented mindset. 
d) Technology and Data Context 
2) Transformation: Data becomes wisdom and influences policy in two iterative and 
interconnected phases: the analytic process and the decision-making process 
3) Outcomes: outcomes in their model indicate what brings value to the whole endeavor. The 
three types of outcomes are: 
a)  Behaviors: Change in behavior to perform data-driven decision-making is not just 
for managerial levels. It depends on the action of the employees who perform the 
works. The people from the downstream must know about the data-based analysis.  
b) Processes and programs: To have a data-driven decision to yield business values, 
it is necessary to adjust the process created in part by an accumulation of behavior 
changes; process changes can be viewed as intermediate outcomes of a specific 
decision. 
c) Financial results: it is essential to specify the expected financial results from 
analytical initiatives to measure the implementation of a data-driven mindset. 
Improved performance, greater profits, reduced costs, or increased market share or 
market value are examples of specific financial outcomes. 
 
==PAGE:31==
 
20 
 
 
Figure 5:A model to building analytical capabilities (Davenport, Harris and Morison 2010) 
 
 The corrective maintenance of conventional decision-making processes can be 
accomplished by creating a data-centric framework reflecting organizations' need to merge and 
incorporate policy and data analysis steps synergistically as a supportive element to their intuition 
and experience. Therefore, decision-makers need to transform data into actionable insight to 
achieve value from their business. Actionable insights involve knowing and analyzing the data, 
manipulating patterns and operations, and turning them into value. “The process of generating 
insights from data generally involves multiple actors from different parts of the organization. Those 
teams' composition and structure are often an outcome of managerial decisions taken within 
existing decision-making routines. Importantly, those routines can both enable and constrain the 
ability of those teams to generate insights. The effects of team composition and existing structures 
on decisions and decision making are subtle but powerful” (Sharma, Mithas and Kankanhalli 2014). 
Francioni et al. (2015) argued that decision-makers' characteristics (competencies, socio-
demographic, and personality) have more effect on converting insights to decisions. However, 
Sharma et al. (2014) argued that converting insights to decision directly relates to decision-making, 
and understanding insights achievement is vital for understanding how business analytics could 
perform efficiently. Accordingly, these researchers argued on two uncertainties that affect 
converting decisions to value: 1) implementing the decisions successfully; 2) success of the 
==PAGE:32==
 
21 
 
strategic action. (Sharma, Mithas and Kankanhalli 2014). Many scholars argued that decision-
makers could use analytics skills to obtain insights and utilize them in their decision-making 
processes. Sharma et al. (2014) stated that a “Good” decision has two specifications: 1) quality: 
indicates objective achievements of decisions; 2) acceptance of the decisions indicate the 
acceptance of subordinates and stakeholders who are responsible for implementing the decisions. 
Mandinach stated it is the organization's responsibility to provide knowledge of DDDM for 
implementers and other stakeholders to obtain acceptance of decisions (E. Mandinach 2012). Most 
of the data-driven organizations investigate much money in providing technological infrastructures 
for collecting and analyzing data. However, these organizations lack the essential analytics skills 
and abilities that decision-makers should capture to interpret these actionable insights in DDDM 
processes. Troisi (2020) proposed that the main dimensions of DDDM are based on five primary 
factors: 
1) Learning organization refers to the strategic necessities of implementing data-driven 
culture. 
2) Technological infrastructure refers to appropriate infrastructure to manage and extract 
useful information from data. 
3) Specialize data analytics skills refers to technical skills. 
4) Proactive process management refers to management skills such as strategic alignment 
with data. 
5) Data management system optimizes data circularity (input, process, output) that affects 
the business value chain (Troisi, et al. 2020). 
 
Mandinach et al. (2014) developed a conceptual DDDM framework for educators' 
decision-making, consisting of three essential elements: data, information, and knowledge. There 
is a process in these elements consist of six cognitive skills: 1) data collection; 2) data organizing; 
3) data analyzing; 4) summarizing; 5) synthesize; 6) prioritize.  
As seen in Figure 6 at the data stage, the two related skills are 'collecting' and 'organizing.' 
In this state, data exists in any format. For maximizing their utilization, they need to be organized 
and ready to transform information. Then there is a need for interpretation after the data are 
structured; therefore, in the next level, the Information 'analyze' and 'summarize.' This helps shape 
information that decisions can be drawn. At the knowledge level, the meaningful data is ready to 
==PAGE:33==
 
22 
 
refer to an action. At this level, 'synthesize' and 'prioritize' skills are essential to decide the direction 
of action. Facts and expertise can eventually be given priority. The synthesis priority helps the 
individual understand future steps and decide which steps should be taken to translate into realistic 
actions based on the situation's experience. Most of these skills can manage by business 
intelligence tools and help decision-makers focus on making decisions and implementing the 
framework's results. (E. Mandinach 2012) 
 
Figure 6: Data-driven decision-making framework (E. Mandinach 2012) 
However, Hall et al. (2015) stated some weaknesses for Mandinach model: 
1) This framework did not include the measurement and review of the execution of 
decisions and the incorporation of decision-making in crucial business processes. 
2) This framework did not consider the external business environment; however, it was 
affected by supply change partners and other stakeholders. (Hall, Jia and Song 2015) 
Based on these lacks, they proposed another framework. These researchers stated that data 
decisions are a continuing process involving data collection, data transmission to final information 
and knowledge, decision-making based on knowledge, tracking decision-making, and feedback 
for each process. Moreover, a company can do not decide individually nor impacts multiple parties, 
including supply chain partners. As illustrated in Figure 7, this model incorporates the target 
organization and combines the supply chain stakeholders and external ecosystems through data 
monitoring and data exchange (Hall, Jia and Song 2015). 
==PAGE:34==
 
23 
 
 
Figure 7:Data-Driven Decision-Making Process (Hall, Jia and Song 2015) 
 
Hall et al. defined “the ability of the organization to use data, knowledge and insight assets in a 
series of organized decision-making processes to help, inform or implement decisions is known as 
data-driven decision-making capability.” (Hall, Jia and Song 2015). These researchers believe that 
the management should develop the five forms of capacity to support their decision-making based 
on data. These researchers, as illustrated in Figure 8, proposed a data-driven decision-making 
capability that comprised: 
Data governance capability: Indicates the organization's ability to "provide users with the 
required degree of precision, timeliness, reliability, protection, confidentiality, connectivity, access 
and ability to tailor data to meet evolving business needs and directions" (Mithas, Ramasubbu and 
==PAGE:35==
 
24 
 
Sambamurthy 2011). This factor consists of collecting data from different sources, integrate the 
collected data; data quality refers to the lifecycle of the data, and data access means make access 
for decision-makers or specific people to accurate data. (This refers to steps 1-3 in Figure 7)  
Data analytic capability: This indicates the organization's ability to extract knowledge from data 
using suitable analytical methods for decision-makers and thus facilitate data-driven decision-
making implementation approaches. Geos (2014) stated that data analytic capability is consist of 
decision time (refers to whether the analytics is performed in real-time, hourly, weekly, monthly, 
or yearly ), analytics ( refers to visualization, exploration, explanatory, and predictive), and 
techniques (reflect the analytics methods or models, such as statistics, econometrics, machine 
learning, computations, linguistics, optimization, and simulation) (Goes 2014). (This refers to 
steps 4-7in Figure 7)   
Insight exploitation capability:  This capability is characterized as the ability of an organization 
to harvest and integrate insights into core business processes, including on six critical business 
processes such as manufacturing/operations activities, marketing, sales, customer service, 
enhancing supplier linkages, and financial management and budgeting (Goes 2014). (This refers 
to steps 6-8 in Figure 7)   
Performance management capability: Refers to establishing a systemic and adequate reporting, 
assessment, and control process to track and assess organizational performance and strategic 
planning through the results ( This refers to steps 10-11 in Figure 7).  
Integration capability: Refers to the organization's capability to integrate the IT infrastructure 
and process and people. IT infrastructure refers to compatible and high-speed knowledge 
transmission. Process refers to coordinate its knowledge flows inside and beyond the organization 
with the relevant parties. People refer to maintain contact and coordination with their staff or 
external collaborators in real-time. 
==PAGE:36==
 
25 
 
 
Figure 8: The Framework of Data-Driven Decision-Making Capability (Hall, Jia and Song 2015) 
A data-driven mentality can also establish a coherent culture and principles that guide data 
processing and organization. Big data analytics capabilities should be involved in complex 
decision-making. Dunn et al. (2013) define the three critical factors in developing skills for 
analytical capability: 
1) knowledge refers to data literacy, analytics skills, and statistical modeling. 
2) Concerns refer to a set of feelings, perceptions, preoccupations, considerations, 
satisfactions, and frustrations related to adopting desired DDDM practices.  
3) Efficacy refers to the decision-makers' convictions of its capacity to participate in 
DDDM effectively. Researchers show that efficiency is a strong forecast for decision-
makers' future actions and a trainable decision-makers' characteristic. 
Recently, Pak and Desimone, in their article, argued about developing principles’ data-
driven decision-making capacity to use data dashboards effectively. These researchers argued that 
it is challenging to engage principles in efforts to build DDDM capabilities. They found four 
leading suggestions: 1) Clearly define the goals for capacity building, 2) Plan for changes in 
professional development culture and alignment, 3) anticipate additional technical requests and 
desires for assistance, 4) build trust in DDDM. (Pak and Desimon 2019). 
==PAGE:37==
 
26 
 
Troisi et al. stated that cognitive computing solutions align with the main features of data-
driven decision-making approaches, especially with data analytic capability elaborated in the 
above sections (Troisi, et al. 2020). Cognitive computing and data analytic capability are 
augmenting data analysis. 
 
For systems built on cognitive computing, the output of big data analytics may be the basis 
for analysis. Chen et al. (2016) stated how cognitive computation operates; it helps compare how 
humans and cognitive technology participate in exploration and decision-making processes. This 
process consists of  
• Observation refers to the fundamental necessity of a cognitive environment where 
data are aggregated, integrated, and reviewed. 
• Interpretation: When many knowledge sources are available, data interpretation can 
improve comprehension and solve complex problems. 
• Evaluation: test how well the solution works and discover novel relationships and 
new hypotheses for further evaluation by synthesizing extant knowledge. 
• Decision-making: refers to the opportunity to create or take data-based action (Chen, 
Argentinis and Weber 2016). 
By combining all features of the decision-making process, big data analysis, and cognitive 
computing system, Troisi et al. proposed the main steps of leading phases of big data through data-
driven decision-making. As depicted in Figure 9, five common macro-dimensions consist of 1) 
Strategy, 2) technological adoption, 3)Technical skills, 4)Management skills and 5)Continuous 
improvement and innovation. (Troisi, et al. 2020) 
==PAGE:38==
 
27 
 
 
Figure 9: The main steps of DDDM: between big data analysis and marketing decision-making (Troisi, et 
al. 2020) 
This model indicates: 
• Companies need to establish strategic goals to bring (strategic sensing and design) 
big data (technology adoption) into the decision-making cycle to ensure the 
alignment between strategy, big data, and decision making.   
• The balance between policy and strategies can be pursued by developing a culture 
that facilitates successful tools between individuals with complementary skills and 
competencies. If correctly and creatively integrated, these capabilities will give rise 
to continuous change and innovation. (Troisi, et al. 2020) 
 
Ugarte stated that defining a data strategy is vital for any data-driven implementation 
(Ugarte 2021).  Saxena stated that data is strategic in nature.  As illustrated in Figure 10, she 
proposed a data strategy framework. Her framework started with the business needs: what is the 
company's mission, strategy, and objective? In the second step, the focus was on the current state 
==PAGE:39==
 
28 
 
of the company: Organizational readiness, business process, Decision-making approaches, and 
data and technology assets. The strategic imperative started by defining the business value targets, 
tactics, and data strategy in the third step. She charactered a data strategy as: 
1- Align with the business goal 
2- Actionable, measurable, relevant 
3- Could identify the critical support, resources, and constraints. 
4- It is an ongoing document 
5-  Add value to the organization 
Finally, the last step is the Actionable plan consisting of planning, project developments, execution, 
and organization readiness. (Saxena 2019) 
 
Figure 10: Data strategy framework ( Source : (Saxena 2019) 
Moreover, Saxena stated that a roadmap consists of four steps for implementing a 
successful data strategy: Educate, engage, explore and execute. She discovered that for 
implementing a successful data strategy, all people in the company should have a common 
understanding of the goal of the data strategy. Encouraging people for effective collaboration by 
involving them in visioning workshops, assess the available data and connect them to business 
requirements, and finally combine business requirements and data assessment to develop a 
roadmap (Saxena 2019). 
2.5 Decision makers characteristics and culture 
The above sections identify that the two essential components of DDDM are: i) tools such 
as data analysis, data mining, reporting, and visualization are essential to fulfill the life cycle of 
the big data; and ii) the human capacity and data literacy is decision-makers ability to interpret 
data. Inadequate data literacy will become an impediment to growth as companies become more 
==PAGE:40==
 
29 
 
data-driven. They decided what automation tools to use to enable decision-makers to access data 
and perform self-service data science and what level of data literacy to attain before using them 
critically (E. Mandinach 2012). The democratization of data in an entire business will make things 
simpler, safer, and wiser for everyone—from CEOs to frontline workers (Dykes 2017). Levin and 
Datnow (2012) characterize a literate data decision-maker as someone who can: 1) consider the 
purpose of data, 2) distinguish good and bad data, 3) understand mathematical and calculation 
principles, 4) prioritize interpretation, and 5) give priority to the use of information, reporting and 
audiences. (Levin and Datnow 2012) 
 Dykes (2017) argued that in data-driven organizations' decision-makers do not need 
advanced data analytical knowledge. However, at a minimum standard, decision-makers can 
efficiently ingest and analyze data. In the following four fields, they would need skills:  
1) Data knowledge: If the decision-makers have enough skills to understand the data as 
business value, they can use them in their decision-making processes—a simple 
understanding of statistical principles and terminology such as their correlation.  
2) Data assimilation:  It means that before consuming unfamiliar data, decision-makers should 
become acquainted with it. There is no need to analyze or judge the data -the only thing 
that needs to be assimilated. (A simple understanding of the title, label, data source, filters, 
sorting, targets) 
3) Data interpretation refers to analyzing and interpreting skills such as pattern, gap analysis, 
Skewness, outliers, etc. 
4) Data skepticism and curiosity: Decision-makers should stand back and consider other 
considerations which are less apparent and which may influence the findings and their 
interpretation and think critically about collected data such as collection methods, context, 
comparations, bias, etc. 
According to other researchers, decision-making characteristics have more dimensions. 
For example, Francioni et al. (2015) identified four main personality characteristics: 
1) Need for achievement refers to people's desire to produce better outcomes and feel 
responsible for their behavior. 
2) Risk attitude refers to the psychological disposition of people to exhibit different levels 
of risk-taking and risk-prevention behavior." 
==PAGE:41==
 
30 
 
3) Socio-demographic characteristics focused more on the level of education and less on 
the age of decision-makers. Glinska-Newes et al. (2020) stated that age is typically 
linked to tenure. However, tenure is argued that affects the specific way a manager has 
in the field. 
4) Competencies refer to the two critical aspects of the decision-makers' roles: i) 
belonging to a network, ii) international experiences. Mandinach stated that decision-
makers' experiences have a high positive correlation to the knowledge about the data. 
(E. Mandinach 2012) 
Carrillo et al. (2019) argued that turning decision-makers into data-driven decision-makers 
is not merely learning a set of analytics skills. It requires developing a "pro-analytics" approach 
that helps connect the various analytical concepts and technologies and trust data analytics results 
for decision-makers. This transformation calls for a cultural shift into business analytics and 
statistics. (Carrillo, et al. 2019) 
Russel Haines worked on demographic and personality variables to identify their influence 
on beliefs, judgments, and moral intent. He defined gender as having the most significant effect 
on ethical decision-making than personal traits (Locus of control and ego strength). His findings 
show that the connection between ethical decision-making factors is dynamic, and various factors 
take on greater importance to determine individuals' moral purpose. (Haines 2007) 
Another approach in this area is the culture and decision-making discussed in an article by 
Yates and Oliveira. These researchers argued that people from different cultures - individualism 
and collectivism- tends to decide differently. The two concepts are somewhat associated with 
collectivist societies tightening them. This can understand why collectivists often weigh more 
feedback from others than individualists in decision making; they can be cautious with adhering 
to standards. (Yatas and Oliveira 2016). These researchers proposed detailed and comprehensive 
anatomy of a decision called “cardinal issue perspective” to describe the ten challenges discussed 
in almost every decision, whether consciously or unconsciously. This cardinal consists of three 
parts: preliminaries, core, and aftermath.  
Preliminaries: refer to What happens before the decision-making process begins?  It 
consists of: 
==PAGE:42==
 
31 
 
 1) Need: Does a decision need to be made or not? Decision-makers compare the DDDM 
and the current decision-making processes and ask why they should switch to DDDM while 
working successfully? 
2)Mode: who will decide, and how will they go about their work?  Decision-makers in 
collectivist cultures are more likely to use DDDM; hence, the tasks are distributed in a team, but 
decision-makers in individualistic cultures prefer to make intuition-based decisions. 
3)Investments: will the decision-maker invest many resources into the decision-making 
process? 
Core: Refers to the issues at the core of the decision process. it consists of  
4) Options: what options are available or could be created?  
5) Possibilities:  what are the possible outcomes associated with each option?  
6) Judgment: What is the likelihood of each outcome occurring? Many decision-makers 
use intuition to conduct descriptive and predictive analytics, regarded as a critical judgment 
component. However, societal barriers would be broken down by training the decision-makers on 
using DDDM procedures to decrease human effort.  
7) Value: How good or bad would each outcome be for the decision-maker (and/or other 
parties)? The management's responsibility is to introduce the vision of transforming to DDDM for 
decision-makers to encourage them to use the analytic techniques to produce valuable insights. 
8) Tradeoffs: How should one manage tradeoffs between options? It refers to the evaluation 
of the potential actions.  
Moreover, aftermath refers to issues in the aftermath:  
9) Acceptability: What do other parties think of the decision? Refers to how engaged the 
other stakeholders in DDDM processes. The objections of decision-makers and other stakeholders 
can be reduced through education and strategic approaches to enforcing a DDDM practice.  
10) Implementation: What can the decision-maker do to ensure that the decision is 
implemented? It refers directly to Hall et al. framework and their concerns about the 
implementation  (Hall, Jia and Song 2015). Hall stated the Mandinach framework's weakness is 
“this framework does not consider the assessment and evaluation of decision implementation and 
the embeddedness of decision-making in core business processes.”.  Hall stated that it is critical 
to raise awareness about DDDM visions at all levels of execution. To ensure correct decisions 
==PAGE:43==
 
32 
 
are taken, it is crucial to evaluate the decisions taken via DDDM compared to decision-makers' 
instincts and experience. 
Ugarte, in his book, stated that besides data-driven culture, there is a need for data-
supported culture, which is slightly different from data-drive culture. (Ugarte 2021). He proposed 
a four-step model for data-support culture consisting of people, processes, providers, and training. 
He believes that these steps are prerequisites to data science.  
 
Figure 11: Data-supported culture (Source: (Ugarte 2021)) 
Ugarte stated it is a common mistake that companies start data-driven by jumping into execution 
and tactics. He stated that the heart of data strategy is based on the 3 Ps: People, Process, and 
Providers, as illustrated in Figure 12. The difference in the pyramid shows the different levels of 
importance in each step. He stated that People, as the most challenging part is the most important, 
is in the first level. Process refers to how people access relevant insights. Finally, providers refer 
to the encompasses software tools.  
==PAGE:44==
 
33 
 
 
Figure 12:   3Ps provide an outline for data strategy 
 
2.6 Summary of the Literature  
The review of the literature demonstrates different aspects of data-driven decision-making. 
The big data characteristics make the organization have two primary categorized data: structured 
and unstructured data. Accordingly, Simon (1960) distinguished processes in programmed and 
nonprogrammed decision-making, and Desouzea and Jacob (2014) believed that data-driven 
decision-making could work better. However, as mentioned in figure 5, humans and machines are 
essential for generating actionable insight from data. Just spending millions of dollars on buying 
technology could not make value without human analytical talent. Many researchers investigated 
defining a framework or a model for implementing data-driven decision-making for organizations. 
( (Davenport, Harris and Morison 2010) (E. Mandinach 2012) (Hall, Jia and Song 2015) (Troisi, 
et al. 2020)). 
One of the first models for implementing the DDDM was Davenport et al. (2010); their 
model's three most essential parts were context, transformation, and outcomes (Figure 5). The 
focus of this model was on the individual's analytics capabilities and used as a fundamental for 
other frameworks. This model could apply to all levels of an organization. Based on the finding of 
this study, in comparison with the other model, this model is mainly like a roadmap. It did not 
specify how to create data, implement behavior change, improve data mindset, manage the external 
environment effect, and measure the effectiveness of the transformation. This model, as mentioned 
==PAGE:45==
 
34 
 
before, is suitable to transform data into knowledge and help to know what steps need to take into 
account when companies want to implement a data-driven approach.  
The Mandinach (2012)  conceptual framework (Figure 6) focuses on the cognitive skills 
used to perform decision-making during three elements: data, information, and knowledge. As Hall 
et al. stated, the lack of this model was on the measurements and reviews of the executions of the 
decisions, and it did not consider the external business environment. Another weakness discovered 
from this study is that the Mandinach model did not consider the strategy, people skills, and culture. 
Just introducing data and knowledge is not enough to be data-driven. The most imperative is how 
to introduce it for decision-makers to use it (Ugarte 2021).    
These lacks were taken into accounts in another framework (Figure 7) defined by Hall et 
al. (2015). However, this model also has some lacks. One of the weaknesses is in strategy. How 
did they align with business goals? The other weakness is in integration. In their model, they had 
integration between supply change and data-driven processes. But how about the integration of 
inside the organization between departments. It is also not clarified where all this produced data 
will be integrated to be accessible for decision-makers. Another weakness is evaluation. The 
evaluation is just for the decisions. However, the evaluation should be applied to BI tools before 
making any decisions. It is also essential to evaluate the data quality to make sure that they are 
trustable for users. Hall et al. did not consider the adoption of the decision-makers for performing 
the DDDM. These researchers did not consider the decision-makers' perspective in their 
framework. It is also helpful to have a monitoring system in the whole process to know what is 
going on in each step. For example, are they align with the strategy?   
  Both frameworks from Mandinach (2012) and Hall et al. (2015) focuses on the needed 
processes for performing data-driven decision-making. Their gap is that they did not have any 
solution for the potential challenges in implementing the processes. 
These weaknesses have been corrected in the Troisi model (Figure 9). Troisi et al. (2020) 
argued that cognitive computing solutions are aligned with the main features of data-driven 
decision-making approaches, especially with data analytic capability. They first defined the main 
DDDM dimensions: Learning orientation and data-driven culture; technological infrastructure; 
specialized analysis skills; process management and proactivity and circularity of the process; and 
==PAGE:46==
 
35 
 
ability to renew knowledge. Then they introduced the big data analysis steps: Data collection and 
organization; data extraction and integration; data analysis; data storage and sharing; and data 
reused. They also used the Hurwitz et al approaches for cognitive computing phases: Observation, 
Interpretation, evaluation, and decision. (Hurwitz, Kaufman and Bowles 2015). Finally, they 
combined all these approaches and defined the five common macro-dimensions of leading big data 
through data-driven decision making, which consist of 1) Strategy, 2) technological adoption, 3) 
Technical skills, 4) Management skills, and 5) Continuous improvement and innovation. Troisi et 
al.'s model identified these stages as the main phases of big data analysis through data-driven 
approaches. Therefore, it looks more completed than the conceptual frameworks of Davenport, 
Mandinach, and Hall et al.. These models have some standard parts: data life cycle, technology, 
and process( Table 3).  However, they have some missing parts, such as decision-makers' 
perspectives.  
 It is clarified from the literature that making value requires that people involved in using 
them should have enough skills and knowledge about data to know how to use them. It is also clear 
that people are always resistant when they need to switch to a new system. However, by defining 
their problem and helping them learn how to use the new tools, it is possible to reduce the 
resistance. Therefore, it is crucial to collect information about the people who will use the new 
tools and, based on their needs, find a solution to encourage them to participate in the new system. 
As illustrated in Table 3, all these models benefit companies aiming to start the 
digitalization processes and help them consider essential aspects to bridge the data strategy and 
business strategy to create value. But how about the companies that already implemented the data-
driven system (partly or wholly) but are still not successful. They faced the most significant 
challenge identified by most of the researchers. They have challenges with people, trust in data, 
the effectiveness of the tools, data strategy, data integration, data modeling, architecture, etc..

This study aims to propose a framework for companies that already implemented the data-
driven approaches but still have challenges in all or some of the identified dimensions, as illustrated 
in Table 3. Companies that already started their journey and implemented some aspects of data-
driven decision-making such as technology, developing tools, producing data, and transforming 
them into actionable insight, the story will be different.  
==PAGE:48==
 
37 
 
Therefore, this study investigated the barriers during the implementation of DDDM and 
how these challenges can be resolved. Another primary focus is to propose a valuable framework 
for implementing the DDDM in a manufacturing company (In the case of Danfoss), which is 
already in the implementation process but has failures. 
 For designing a contingency framework for a company, it is essential to analyze all aspects 
of implementation. Therefore, the interview questions are based on these identified dimensions 
and elements. This means that by realizing the barriers from interviews, analyzing the current 
process for decision-making in the company, and previous literature, it is possible to design a  new 
framework. This will be analyzed in chapter 4, and a solution will be presented in chapter 5, where 
the proposed framework will be discussed. 
  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:49==

CHAPTER 3 
3 Research Methodology  
 
CHAPTER INTRODUCTION 
This chapter is involved: 3.1 Research Process: provides the method and the research 
approach, 3.2 Data Collection: describe the theoretical samplings of the research,3.3 Questioner 
Design and Interviewees Profiles: described the generation of interview questions and introduced 
the interviewee's profile, 3.4 Data Analysis: refers to the generation of the final solution. 
 
3.1 Research Process 
To address the critical research question “How achieve data-driven decision-making 
approaches in manufacturing companies which has challenges in the implementation phase 
of data-driven approaches?“ it is essential to have a structured research process and timetable. 
Therefore, as illustrated in Figure 13, the research methodology is Design science Research 
(Janssen, Van Der Voort and Wahyudi 2017). The process started by defining a problem which 
leads to formulating the purpose and the research questions. Then, the literature review and some 
meetings with Analytics & Insights Manager performed by the author offered a forum for defining 
the project scope where the implementation of the data-driven decision making has been identified 
as a core problem in the Danfoss climate solution department. Next, data collection includes both 
empirical and theoretical data to find a solution for the defined problem. Finally, the collected data 
is analyzed, and the final solution is generated in the form of a framework and recommendation. 
The scheduled timetable is shown in Appendices 1: Project Timetable. 
 
==PAGE:50==
 
39 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3.2 Data Collection 
Based on the literature review, a qualitative study design is used to investigate the 
implementation of data-driven decision-making and its contribution to the decision-making 
process to address the research's issue statement. Therefore, a series of semi-structured interviews 
(empirical data collection) with Danfoss managers who actively engage in decision-making were 
performed. Based on the findings from the pilot meeting with Analytics and Insights teams in 
section 1.4, Danfoss already implemented their BI tools for their daily activities. Therefore, they 
know that they are in the process of switching to data-driven approaches. Nevertheless, some 
Define 
problem
